[M─Бtс╣Ыn─Бman.тАФс╣гaс╕Нviс╣Е┼Ыam. m─Бtс╣Ыn─Бm─Бdevatyam uta mantroktadevatyam. ─Бnuс╣гс╣нubham: 2.purast─Бdbс╣Ыhat─л; 10. 3-av. 6-p. jagat─л; 11, 12, 14, 16. pathy─Бpan╠Дkti; 15. 3-av. 7-p. ┼Ыakvar─л br─Бhmaс╣Зaspaty─Б; 17. tath─Б jagat─л.]

Comment

Weber (and Zimmer after him: p. 321) conjectures that the two ‘winners of a husband ’ are the breasts, and that the reference is to the ceremonious washing of the young child. The numerous names of evil beings in the hymn are in good part unknown elsewhere and untranslatable; of some of them tentative versions can be added in parenthesis. The comm. reads al─л┼Ыas in d, and explains it as ali + ─л┼Ыa тМКtaking ali as diseases (or deities representing them) that fly about like beesтМЛ.

A charm to exercise evil spirits who beset women

Translation
  1. The two spouse-finders which thy mother rubbed up for thee when born
    (fem.)тАФfor them тМКt├бtraтМЛ let not the ill-named one be greedy, the
    aliс╣Е┼Ыa nor the vats├бpa (calf-drinker or -protector?).
Notes

Weber (and Zimmer after him: p. 321) conjectures that the two ‘winners
of a husband ’ are the breasts, and that the reference is to the
ceremonious washing of the young child. The numerous names of evil
beings in the hymn are in good part unknown elsewhere and
untranslatable; of some of them tentative versions can be added in
parenthesis. The comm. reads al─л┼Ыas in d, and explains it as ali

  • ─л┼Ыa тМКtaking ali as diseases (or deities representing them) that
    fly about like beesтМЛ.
Griffith

Let neither fiend of evil name, Alinsa, Vatsapa, desire Thy pair of husband-wooers which thy mother cleansed when, thou wast born.

Translation
  1. He who lies with (ni-pad) thee in sleep, having become [like] a
    brother and like a fatherтАФthem, eunuch-formed, tiara-decked (tir─лс╣н├нn),
    let the baj├б force (sah) from here.
Notes

Tir─лс╣нin = ‘womanish,’ as wearing a distinctive woman’s head-dress; the
comm., however, paraphrases the word with antardh─Бnen─Б тАЩс╣нataс╕е, as if
from tiraс╕е-aс╣нin! Ppp. puts the verse after vs. 8, and reads supt─Бс╣Б
for svapne in a, and, in c, d, vajas taс╣Б…kl─лbar┼лpaс╣Б
kir─лс╣нinam. тМКCf. RV. x. 162. 5-6 with our vss. 7-8.тМЛ

Griffith

Whoever, in thy brother’s shape or father’s comes to thee in sleep, Let Baja rout and chase them like eunuchs with woman’s head- dress on.

Whitney
Translation
  1. He who surprises (tsar) thee sleeping, who tries to harm thee
    wakingтАФthem the circling (pari-kram) sun hath made to vanish away like
    a shadow.
Notes

Both translators understand the second half-verse to mean ’them hath the
baja made to vanish, as the sun the shadow,’ and the comm. takes it in
the same way; but, though that may be the virtual sense, it is not what
the line actually says. Ppp. reads, in a, supt─Бс╣Б chinatti, with
ca for tv─Б in b. The comm. has carati instead of tsarati in
a. Nearly all our mss. (all save D.R.) read j─Б╠Бgralim at end of
b, but SPP. strangely reports no such variant from any of his
authorities.

Griffith

Whoever steals to thee asleep or thinks to harm thee when awake,– These hath it banished, as the Sun travelling round drives shade away.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Whoever makes this woman one having a dead child (-vast├б), or a
    miscarriage, him, O herb, do thou make disappear, lustful тМКaccusativeтМЛ
    for her, slippery.
Notes

The last p─Бda is very obscure and doubtful, and quite otherwise
understood by the translators; the version given follows the Petersburg
Lexicon. The comm. interprets kamalam by garbhadv─Бram, and a├▒jivam
by abhivyaktimad mlakс╣гaс╣Зopetaс╣Б v─Б, and supplies to them kuru. Ppp.
reads instead kamalavaс╣Б dyuvam; it also reverses the order of
mс╣Ыt├бvats─Бm and ├бvatok─Бm in a, b. A├▒jiv├бm (p. a├▒jiре░v├бm) is
quoted under Pr─Бt. iv. 18 as an example of a word made with a
*taddhita-*suffix beginning with v.

Griffith

Whoever causeth her to lose her child or bear untimely fruit,– Destroy him, O thou Plant, destroy the slippery fiend who lusts for her.

  1. They who dance around the dwellings (┼Ы─Б╠Бl─Б) in the evening, making
    donkey-noisesтАФthey that [are] kus┼л╠Бlas (granaries) and kukс╣гil├бs

Griffith

Those who at evening, with the bray of asses, dance around the house, Kukshilas, and Kusfilas, and Kakubhas, Srimas, Karumas, These with thine odour, O thou Plant, drive far away to every side.

Whitney
Translation
  1. The kuk├║ndhas, the kuk┼л╠Бrabhas, that bear skins (kс╣Ы╠Бtti), pelts
    (? d┼лr┼Ы├б), dancing on like impotent men, that make a noise in the
    forestтАФthem we make disappear from here.
Notes

Ppp. reads kakundh─Бс╣г kar┼лrabh─Бс╣г kс╣Ыty─Бir duri┼Ы─Бni bibhrati: kl─лv─Бi тАЩva
pr. ghoс╣г─Бс╣Б ye kurvate vane. The comm. has kс╣Ыkandh─Бс╕е kuk┼лrav─Бс╕е kс╣Ыty─Бir
d┼лс╣гy─Бni.

Griffith

Kukundhas and Kukurabhas who dress themselves in hides and skins, Who dance about like eunuchs, who raise a wild clamour in the wood, all these we banish far away.

Whitney
Translation
  1. They who do not endure yonder sun, burning down from the sky, the
    niggards, buck-clothed, ill-smelling, red-mouthed, the m├бkakas, we
    make to disappear.
Notes

Ppp. reads in c r─Бyaс╣Б vastav─Бsino, and, in e, mс╣Ыс╣гak─Бn for
makak─Бn. In bastav─Бsin, Weber understands -v─Бsin as ‘smelling,’
the Petersburg Lexicons (also Ludwig alternatively) as ‘bleating’ (for
-v─Б┼Ыin); the comm. avicarinavasan─Бn. тМКFor the verse-sequence in
Ppp., see above.тМЛ

Griffith

All those who cannot bear the Sun who warms us yonder from the sky, Arayas with the smell of goats, malodorous, with bloody mouths, the Makakas we drive afar.

Whitney
Translation
  1. They who go before a woman, bearing horns (pl.) in the hand, stayers
    in the oven, laughing out, who make light in the tuftтАФthem we make to
    disappear from here.
Notes

All our mss., and nearly all those of SPP., read badhv├аs in a, and
our edition follows them. SPP. gives in his text vadhv├аs, with the
commentator. In c, he adopts ─Бp─Бkeс╣гс╣нh─Б╠Бs, with a small minority of
his mss., and directly against Pr─Бt. ii. 94, which prescribes -sth─Б╠Бs.
One would like to emend to ap─Бk- ‘standing aloof.’

Griffith

Those, bearing horns upon their hands, who first of all approach the brides; Standing in ovens, laughing loud, those who in bushes flash forth light, all these we banish hence away.

Translation
  1. Of whom the front-feet are behind, the heels in front, the faces in
    front, who are threshing-floor-born, dung-smoke-born, who are ├║ruс╣Зс╕Нas
    and maс╣нmaс╣н├бs, pot-testicled, ay─Б┼Ы├║s (impotent?)тАФthese from her, O
    Brahmaс╣Зaspati, do thou make to disappear by attention (? prat─лbodh├б).
Notes

Ppp. reads in c ┼Ы─Бkadh-, in d ye ca mayyaj─Б, and in e
combines -с╣гk─Б тАЩy─Б┼Ы-. Some of our mss. (Bp.P.M.W.), as of SPP’s, read
m├║kh─Бс╕е at end of ‘b. The comm. has, for d, aruс╣Зс╕Н─Б ye ca
muс╣нmuс╣н─Бс╕е; he explains ay─Б┼Ыavas by ayo v─Бyur v─Бyuvad ─Б┼Ыug─Бminaс╕е.

Griffith

Those who have retroverted toes, and heels and faces in the front, Khalajas, Sakadhumajas, Urundas, all the Matmatas, impotent Kumbhamushkas, these, Drive thou, O Brahmanaspati, far from this girl with vigilance.

Notes

Doubtless the concluding word should be spandan─Б╠Б, as given by SPP., in
accordance, as he claims, with all but two of his authorities. The mss.
are always so careless and untrustworthy in their distinction of sp
and sy that it must be the sense rather than their testimony that
decides in any case which is the true reading. Ppp. appears to have
syandan─Б. The combination up├йс╣гantam (p. upaре░├йс╣гantam: Ppp.
upe┼Ыantam) is according to Pr─Бt. iii. 52; the passage is quoted in the
commentary to that rule: the comm. тМКand his textтМЛ read here up─Бiс╣г-.
The comm. also has ┼Ы─Бlaс╕Нam (Ppp. ┼Ы─Бl┼лс╕Нham) in d, and takes pr├б
vidhya (Ppp. pravс╣Ыddhi) in e as prav├нdhya, gerund. Further, he
has pr─Бsy─Бt instead of p─Бrс╣гс╣Зy─Б, and, at the end, spandan─Бt. His
verse-division is different from ours, as he reckons e, f to vs. 18.
He explains mar─лmс╣Ы┼Ыam in b as = punaс╕еpunar mс╣Ы┼Ыantam. Ppp. has,
for udumbalaс╣Б tuс╣Зс╕Нelam, adaraс╣Б sulatuс╣Зс╕Нenam.

Griffith

The Bristly-haired, the Maniac-haired, the Biter, and the Groper-fiend, The Creeper-near, the Copper-hued, the Snouty, and the Saluda, With foot and heel kick over, as a hasty cow her milking-pan.

    bearing [women]тАФthe Gandharvas, woman-seekers (?), let the brown one

Whitney
Translation
  1. Let [her] maintain what is left (?); what is set, let not that
    fall down; let the two formidable remedies, to be borne in the under
    garment, defend thine embryo.
Notes

Parisс╣Ыс╣гс╣нam in a is very hard to deal with, both on account of the
meaning and because combinations of root sс╣Ыj with pari are hardly
met with; the Pet. Lexicon suggests emendation to -┼Ыriс╣гс╣нam; as both
Ppp. and the comm. have -┼Ыiс╣гс╣нam, I have taken the liberty of so
translating. The comm. paraphrases it by hom─Бdiviniyog─Бva┼Ыiс╣гс╣нaс╣Б
sarс╣гapadvayam тМКsee the introductionтМЛ and makes it the object of
dh─Бrayatu. Ppp. also reads yujyatam for yad dhitam in b, and,
at the end, nivabh─Бryay─Бu. тМКWhitney queries for a: ‘Let what is
wreathed about (pari-sс╣Ыj) maintain.’ This might refer to bandages
swathed around, to support the abdomen. In b, hit├бm would refer to
the embryo (cf. dh─Бt─Б dadh─Бtu etc.), and ├бva p─Бdi to untimely
delivery (cf. i. 11. 4-6).тМЛ

Griffith

Let it maintain the genial seed: let the laid embryo rest secure. Let both strong Healers, to be worn within the girdle, guard the babe.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Childlessness, still-birth, also crying, guilt (agh├б), barrenness
    (? ─Бvay├б)тАФthat do thou attach to [our] enemy (├бpriya), as if
    having made a garland from a tree.
Notes

Ppp. reads, in a, b, m─Бrtavatsam ─Бm─Бbhrogham agham ─Бnayam. The
comm. has, for agham ─Бvayam, aghav─Бvayam, and paraphrases it with
agh─Бn─Бm p─Бp─Бn─Бс╣Б tatphalabh┼лt─Бn─Бс╣Б duс╕еkh─Бn─Бс╣Б v─Б тАЩsakс╣Ыd vayanam. It is
curious that both ─Б-vayam and m─Бrta-vatsam are quoted in the
commentary to Pr─Бt. iv. 18, as if their second member were ‘a taddhita
beginning with v.’тАФтМКCf. MB. i. 1. 14; MP. i. 4. 11.тМЛ

тМКHere ends the third anuv─Бka, with 2 hymns and 48 verses. It is also
the end of the sixth artha-s┼лkta, which begins with y─Б├║ te. The
quoted Anukr. says y─Бu te.тМЛ

From this point on, the commentary is wanting until the beginning of
book xi. 7.

Griffith

Sterility, and infants’ death, and weeping that announceth woe, Dear! lay them on the fiend as thou wouldst pluck a garland from a tree.

рдЯрд┐рдкреНрдкрдгреА: реиремтИТ(рдЕрдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдореН) рдирд┐рддреНрдпрдорд╕рд┐рдЪреН рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рдореЗрдзрдпреЛрдГред рдкрд╛реж релредрекредрезреиреиред рдЕрдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛-рдЕрд╕рд┐рдЪреН, рднрд╛рд╡реЗ рддреНрд╡, рдЫрд╛рдиреНрджрд╕реЛ рджреАрд░реНрдШрдГред рдЕрдкреНрд░рдЬрд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдореНред рд╕рдиреНрддрд╛рдирд░рд╛рд╣рд┐рддреНрдпрдореН (рдорд╛рд░реНрддрд╡рддреНрд╕рдореН) рднрд╛рд╡реЗ рдЕрдгреНред рдореГрддрдмрд╛рд▓рддреНрд╡рдореН (рдЖрддреН) рдЕрдкрд┐ рдЪ (рд░реЛрджрдореН) рд░реБрджрд┐рд░реН рдЕрд╢реНрд░реБрд╡рд┐рдореЛрдЪрдиреЗ-рдШрдЮреНред рд░реЛрджрдирдореН (рдЕрдШрдореН) рдкрд╛рдкрдореН (рдЖрд╡рдпрдореН) рдЖ+рд╡+рдпрдореНред рд╡рд╛ рдЧрддрд┐рдЧрдиреНрдзрдирдпреЛрдГ-рдб, рдпреБрдЬрд┐рд░реН рдпреЛрдЧреЗ-рдбред рдЖ рд╕рдордиреНрддрд╛рджреН рд╡рд╕реНрдп рдЧрдиреНрдзрдирд╕реНрдп рд╣рд┐рдВрд╕рдирд╕реНрдп рдпрдВ рдпреЛрдЧрдореН (рд╡реГрдХреНрд╖рд╛рддреН) рджреНрд░реБрдорд╛рддреН (рдЗрд╡) рдпрдерд╛ (рд╕реНрд░рдЬрдореН) рдЕреж резредрезрекредрезред рдкреБрд╖реНрдкрдорд╛рд▓рд╛рдореН (рдХреГрддреНрд╡рд╛) рдирд┐рд░реНрдорд╛рдп (рдЕрдкреНрд░рд┐рдпреЗ) рджреНрд╡реЗрд╖реНрдпреЗ (рдкреНрд░рддрд┐ рдореБрдЮреНрдЪ) рдкреНрд░рддреНрдпрдХреНрд╖рдВ рдореЛрдЪрдп (рддрддреН) рдкреВрд░реНрд╡реЛрдХреНрддрдВ рдХрд░реНрдо рее
